  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

Their mother always liked Angela better, and Turtle lacked confidence because of that. The greatest challenge she confronted was her confidence in herself. Maybe the book doesn't support this, but I do. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62988894" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62988894?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780380679911" href="/book/show/763477.The_Westing_Game">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=mark-harmon">mark-harmon</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62988894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15483128486434863171">this is what i am going to do: i am going to take a red panda, and i am going to learn genetics and i dunno - neuroscience. and welding. and i am going to take a little bit of my brain, and a little bit of everyone's brain here on goodreads.com (you'll be asleep, you wont feel a thing) and then i am going to moosh it all together, and put it in the brain of the red panda. and then i will have the perfect book-recommending resource. because if i had had one of these when i was little, then it wou</span>
  <span id="freeText15483128486434863171" style="display:none">this is what i am going to do: i am going to take a red panda, and i am going to learn genetics and i dunno - neuroscience. and welding. and i am going to take a little bit of my brain, and a little bit of everyone's brain here on goodreads.com (you'll be asleep, you wont feel a thing) and then i am going to moosh it all together, and put it in the brain of the red panda. and then i will have the perfect book-recommending resource. because if i had had one of these when i was little, then it would have told me, "you love peggy parrish and her wordplay-based mysteries and you have seen the movie <i>clue</i> enough times that you can recite the whole thing (still can). here's a book you will like". i would have to fine tune it so it works better than the one they have on amazon.com or netflix.com (because, no, i would not like to see <i>the aviator</i>, thank you). i would have loved this book like crazy as a kid. as a grown up, i liked it very much, but thought the characters could have used a little fleshing out to make them more defined. the child-me would not have cared. now i have to go write 250 academic words about it. so much less fun than mad scientisting.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>

  <span id="freeText10480021076815870403" style="display:none">Really fun, and I know without hesitation what third or fourth grade me loved about it. I felt like maybe it dragged a tiny bit in the back half of the second act, but I think that's just me getting ahead of the narrative, and being a little out of the demo.<br /><br />But if you're like 11 to 13? Holy crap, you're gonna love this book, and be on board with it pretty much from the first chapter. It was written in 1978, but it doesn't feel dated (other than the technology), and it ages very well. It has a diverse cast, which I didn't appreciate as a kid, because I didn't know any better, but which I deeply appreciate now. Much of it reads as brief vignettes, which made it very easy to pick up and put down.<br /><br />I highly recommend this for young readers, and I highly recommend it a second time to adults who read it when we were kids.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11849139081666740498">This book sounded like it would be lots of fun, and I read it hoping for a great mystery. In the end I think there were too many characters, and not enough information to make any of them seem real to me. I never really got why they were who they were, except on the most basic level. Each character was just glossed over, and even though they were described in a basic way, there was nothing to really draw me in or make me care about them.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_110528426" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110528426" />
    <a title="carol. " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3978225-carol"><img alt="carol. " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110528426?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780525423201" href="/book/show/518856.The_Westing_Game">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=awards">awards</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=female-lead">female-lead</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=my-library-hardcover">my-library-hardcover</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110528426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2270823854137252558"><i>The Westing Game</i> is first full-length mystery I remember reading. Well, besides <i>Encyclopedia Brown </i>and <i>Nancy Drew</i> books. But the one mystery that I could still have told you general details about the plot. It might have been the cleverness of the mystery or  it's absence of gore. It could have been identification with the shin-kicking protagonist, nicknamed 'Turtle.' It could have been the clever signals of winds and atmosphere that run throughout the book. Whatever it was, Raskin's story stayed</span>
  <span id="freeText2270823854137252558" style="display:none"><i>The Westing Game</i> is first full-length mystery I remember reading. Well, besides <i>Encyclopedia Brown </i>and <i>Nancy Drew</i> books. But the one mystery that I could still have told you general details about the plot. It might have been the cleverness of the mystery or  it's absence of gore. It could have been identification with the shin-kicking protagonist, nicknamed 'Turtle.' It could have been the clever signals of winds and atmosphere that run throughout the book. Whatever it was, Raskin's story stayed with me for years.<br /><br />Opening page:<br /><b><br />"The sun sets in the west (just about everyone knows that), but Sunset Towers faced east. Strange!<br />Sunset Towers faced east and had no towers. This glittery, glassy apartment house stood alone on the Lake Michigan shore five stories high. Five empty stories high.<br />Then one day (it happened to be the Fourth of July), a most uncommon-looking delivery boy rode around town slipping letters under the doors of the chosen tenants-to-be. The letters were signed <i>Barney Northrup.</i><br />The delivery boy was sixty-two years old, and there was no such person as Barney Northrup."<br /></b><br />It is a variation on the manor house mystery, with a very disparate group of people brought together physically. Initially, they are convinced to rent or buy units in the newly constructed Sunset Towers, a small building that has room for a coffee shop, a restaurant and a small office, perfect for further enticing the future tenants. The tenants discover they have something additional in common when they are called together for the reading of Sam Westing's will. An isolating snowstorm ramps up the tension.<br /><br />Narration is third person, which is solidly done. Initially, all the characters have aspects that make them seem flawed, or perhaps somewhat unlikable. Interestingly, however, it was probably one of the broadest casts I can remember reading: a black woman who is now a judge, who grew up poor; a Greek family, whose skin is 'darker' than the black woman's (an interesting concept for a young white kid!); a Chinese family, one a recent immigrant; a couple of economically limited white guys; a suburban white family; a single white older woman dressmaker. We pop in and out of most of their heads at some point, which ends up giving the reader more insight than they each have on each other.<br /><br />There's accusations in a review or two of racism, but on adult read, I'd say that the racism is all internal to the characters, and Raskin does a solid job of showing how things a certain character might say or do regarding someone else's race is about their own knowledge deficits. I found only a couple of moments for me that might not pass the twenty-first century sniff test: One of the characters, Chris, has some sort of unspecified physical disability that impairs movement and speech. One of the questionable moments comes up when his brother, Theo, tells someone else that they don't need to talk to Chris like a baby, "because he's not retarded."<br /><br />I usually avoid reading books from childhood, as I'm afraid of having precious memories tarnished. I thought <i>The Westing Game</i> held up well. It's told in an omniscient third person, and tends to switch person and location fairly frequently. In the book, the switches are clearly denoted with ****, but it's the sort of thing that probably won't translate well to audio, unless it was done with an ensemble cast.<br /><br /> I think it is definitely a YA, but in the best sense of the word. Many of the techniques it uses are great for people that are younger and haven't figured some of this out yet; ie. a judge that still has some insecurities, or decides that she will not to compete for the prize, but to protect. The shifts in perspective and time work well for developing empathy--I think each character goes through a redemption arc, and even the one I remember disliking the most--Otis--was shown to be something other than appearance suggested. I ended up searching out a hardcover for my own library, and am glad to have it around.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2270823854137252558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_37806564" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37806564" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1719442-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451874923p2/1719442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37806564?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/1719442-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">elementary girls with long hair</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=children-s">children-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=bc-before-college">bc-before-college</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=sisters-book-club">sisters-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=col-mustard-in-the-parlor">col-mustard-in-the-parlor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37806564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13451060855052061341"> I think I first read The Westing Game in third or fourth grade. I checked it out of a public school library in Missouri. I loved it, returned it, and checked it again a few months later on another weekly library visit. Two things: 1. Why should children only go to the library once a week? My education would have been brighter and fuller had I just stayed in the library. Other kids could have had more time with the restroom pass, but instead I hoarded that thing and sat on the white raised seat </span>
  <span id="freeText13451060855052061341" style="display:none"> I think I first read The Westing Game in third or fourth grade. I checked it out of a public school library in Missouri. I loved it, returned it, and checked it again a few months later on another weekly library visit. Two things: 1. Why should children only go to the library once a week? My education would have been brighter and fuller had I just stayed in the library. Other kids could have had more time with the restroom pass, but instead I hoarded that thing and sat on the white raised seat reading away. I'm sure my teacher must've been concerned over my restroom needs/habits. 2.I loved Turtle, the girl with the braids that beg to be pulled. I braided my hair like Turtle's and liked whirling around and using them as weapons against boys coming in for the kisschase win. Which was a good development because a couple of years earlier I bit Rashad Ware when he lumbered towards me for a smooch. (I told my parents that I didn't bite him; just was running with my mouth open and happened to want to close it when his arm showed up)<br /><br /> Back to the book. Still, years later, in love with Turtle, only the mother in me now has room to love Flora Baumbach, hair braider, as well. And Mrs. stickyfingers Hoo, my new favorite. So, still in love with the book. The whizzbang puzzle mystery abides, only the clues are not as mysterious and I did wish that purple waves meant something really, really sinister and twisted. But that's just my maturity showing. So great to be grown up.<br /> This was a sister book club pick. My youngest sister had never read it; apparently, an epic fail in my big sister job on that one. But, it's good to see that I did well enough a job that she knew to find it herself and suggest it for book club. I've helped raise a responsible adult. Even if she liked Crow, the woman in black. <br /> My middle sister took a long time, too long of a time, to read this book. But she finished, liked it, and all is well. <br /> This would be a great family read aloud book. But, to stop my eldest from reading ahead I would have to hide it really well. Maybe even have clues. And a wax dummy dead body! Long live the Westing Game. <br /><br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13451060855052061341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3087458471" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3087458471" />
    <a title="Evgeny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny"><img alt="Evgeny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544797852p2/9340136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3087458471?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3087458471" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1808812360538749394"><b>A group read with my pantaloonless buddies.</b><br/><br/>Sixteen very different people were invited to reading of a will of multi-millionaire Samuel W. Westing. <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576979890i/28650615.jpg' width='300' height='300' alt='Millionaire' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>The guy stated he would be killed and whoever finds the murderer would get all his fortune. He provided the participants (or players as Westing called them) with different clues to help, but in my humble opinion they only provided additional confusion. <br/><br/>Coming back in time somebody worked really hard to make sure these sixteen people come to live in th</span>
  <span id="freeText1808812360538749394" style="display:none"><b>A group read with my pantaloonless buddies.</b><br><br>Sixteen very different people were invited to reading of a will of multi-millionaire Samuel W. Westing. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576979890i/28650615.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="Millionaire" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The guy stated he would be killed and whoever finds the murderer would get all his fortune. He provided the participants (or players as Westing called them) with different clues to help, but in my humble opinion they only provided additional confusion. <br><br>Coming back in time somebody worked really hard to make sure these sixteen people come to live in the same luxurious apartment building. If you think this beginning has a strong And Then There Were None vibe, you are absolutely right. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576980194i/28650643._SX540_.jpg" alt="And Then There Were None" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Again, in my humble opinion Ellen Raskin does not come close to the greatness of Agatha Christie. Then again, who does?<br><br>Reading this book clued me in on how to spot a YA book easily. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576980327i/28650655.jpg" alt="YA" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The biggest giveaway would be the fact that in this genre slightly grown-up kids (aka young adults) are always unfailingly smarter than the actual adults. The more obnoxious the teen, the more smarter she/he turns out to be. I can understand it: in children books the adults are treated as guardians, teachers, etc. and thus they are not brain-dead. If you want to ponder to teenagers with their rebellious phase of growing up (we all came through this), you want to show them to be the smart ones. I can accept this. By the way, in case you know of an exception to this rule please inform me as I cannot think of any. <br><br>What I do not accept is the condescending tone of YA literature. For some reason in children books the readers often treated as equals making them better and more satisfying reads for me - an adult who left his teen years behind a long time ago. The book was guilty of this.<br><br>Last piece of criticism. I hate rapid changes of POVs with the passion. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576980437i/28650661._SX540_.jpg" alt="POV" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Here they would often change after just one paragraph making the whole story look like a series in disconnected scenes. I like the idea of "tell, do not show", but here is was taken to the extreme adding to the feeling of disconnection between what was actually shown and not told. <br><br>However after all the mud slinging I did, I liked the book well enough not to get too bored (I only got frustrated from time to time) and I have not regrets about reading it. The rating is 3 stars. Anti-YA rant mode off. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1808812360538749394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18251697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18251697" />
    <a title="Isaac Blevins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1006379-isaac-blevins"><img alt="Isaac Blevins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206072236p2/1006379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18251697?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Isaac Blevins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Isaac Blevins" href="/user/show/1006379-isaac-blevins">Isaac Blevins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">children, children at heart, puzzle fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1006379-isaac-blevins?shelf=ya-literature">ya-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18251697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6214404039174923430">I read this little book for the first time not as a child - but as an adult. I was looking for a book to kick off our Junior High book club and picked up the Westing Game to see if it might be a good place to begin. <br/> I wish that I had found this book earlier in my life. What kid wouldn't be captivated by wonderful characters thrown together to play a game hosted by a dead millionaire? Don't get me wrong...Mr. Westing isn't a vampire or a zombie - he's just decided that his heirs need to do a lit</span>
  <span id="freeText6214404039174923430" style="display:none">I read this little book for the first time not as a child - but as an adult. I was looking for a book to kick off our Junior High book club and picked up the Westing Game to see if it might be a good place to begin. <br /> I wish that I had found this book earlier in my life. What kid wouldn't be captivated by wonderful characters thrown together to play a game hosted by a dead millionaire? Don't get me wrong...Mr. Westing isn't a vampire or a zombie - he's just decided that his heirs need to do a little puzzle solving in order to earn their share of his estate. While the mystery and the puzzles are fun and wonderfully clever, it's the characters that really make this novel.<br /> All of the characters reside and work in the same high rise apartment building within view of the looming Westing estate. Getting snowed in with them is like being trapped with the most interesting people you could imagine - both good and bad. By the end of the novel it's almost like you're part of a family reunion you know these people so well.<br /><br />Do yourself a favor - if you're a kid: pick up this book and have a wonderful time!<br />- if you're an adult: pick up this book and enjoy being a kid again!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6214404039174923430" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3042218487" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3042218487" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer3432408414462296771">The Westing Game, Ellen Raskin<br/>The Westing Game is a mystery book written by Ellen Raskin in 1978. Sunset Towers is a new apartment building on Lake Michigan, north of Milwaukee and just down the shore from the mansion owned by reclusive self-made millionaire Samuel W. Westing. As the story opens, Barney Northrup is selling apartments to a carefully selected group of tenants. He claims that chess is not allowed in the building. This is a big clue, as Sam Westing loved chess. After Sam Westing die</span>
  <span id="freeText3432408414462296771" style="display:none">The Westing Game, Ellen Raskin<br />The Westing Game is a mystery book written by Ellen Raskin in 1978. Sunset Towers is a new apartment building on Lake Michigan, north of Milwaukee and just down the shore from the mansion owned by reclusive self-made millionaire Samuel W. Westing. As the story opens, Barney Northrup is selling apartments to a carefully selected group of tenants. He claims that chess is not allowed in the building. This is a big clue, as Sam Westing loved chess. After Sam Westing dies, at the beginning of the book, it emerges that most of the tenants are named as heirs in Westing's will. The will is structured as a puzzle, with the 16 heirs challenged to find the solution. Each of the eight pairs, assigned seemingly at random, is given $10,000 cash and a different set of baffling clues. The pair that solves the mystery will inherit Westing's entire $200 million fortune and control of his company. It is discovered that Berthe Erica Crow is the answer but not the murderer. In the end, unknown to the other players, Turtle Wexler wins the game and inherits Sam Westing's company.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش نسخه اصلی: روز سی ام ماه آوریل سال 2019 میلدی<br />عنوان: تو را در بازی کشتند؛ نویسنده: الن راسکین ؛ مترجم: آیدا پورنگ‌فر؛ تهران: نشر خزه، ‏‫ 1398؛ در 288 ص؛ شابک: 9786229581810؛‬ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان ایالات متحده آمریکا - سده 20 م<br /><br />ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12242348326336840368">This book was delightful. <br/><br/>I'm still reeling from the fact that the author wrote this straight through without knowing all the plot details or how everything would turn out. Sam Westing dies and leaves behind one final game for his 16 heirs who he has move into the same apartment building. A bit Agatha Christie mixed in and you are reading about this puzzle. Everyone is not who they say they are and the author slowly unravels the mystery.<br/><br/>For some reason, this took me far longer to read that it sh</span>
  <span id="freeText12242348326336840368" style="display:none">This book was delightful. <br /><br />I'm still reeling from the fact that the author wrote this straight through without knowing all the plot details or how everything would turn out. Sam Westing dies and leaves behind one final game for his 16 heirs who he has move into the same apartment building. A bit Agatha Christie mixed in and you are reading about this puzzle. Everyone is not who they say they are and the author slowly unravels the mystery.<br /><br />For some reason, this took me far longer to read that it should have. It was a quick page turner and the paragraphs switched perspectives almost from sentence to sentence, which made it a little tricky to follow along. There were a lot of characters, which also made it hard to keep them all straight, especially the families. I really enjoyed getting to know how everything played out, and especially the time gaps in the ending. Great book for almost every reader.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2055966140679521365">I don't understand why this book won a Newbery Award. It was confusing and sort of awful. Additionally, for today's reader, it felt extremely dated and had some remarks in it that I would call "un-politically correct."<br/>It all starts with a group of eccentric people of all ages who quickly become involved in a mystery game involving a large inheritance. The person who first solves the mystery wins the inheritance. Clues are given along the way, but I'm not sure whether or not the reader was suppos</span>
  <span id="freeText2055966140679521365" style="display:none">I don't understand why this book won a Newbery Award. It was confusing and sort of awful. Additionally, for today's reader, it felt extremely dated and had some remarks in it that I would call "un-politically correct."<br />It all starts with a group of eccentric people of all ages who quickly become involved in a mystery game involving a large inheritance. The person who first solves the mystery wins the inheritance. Clues are given along the way, but I'm not sure whether or not the reader was supposed to be able to solve the mystery. I wouldn't recommend this and I feel bad for kids who have to read it as an assignment.</span>

    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1223081223?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1223081223" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10315808100236071795">This was one of my favorite children's books, and I decided to reread it by listening to it on audio. What a delight! The mystery held up really well, and Diane the Adult had fun following the clues being dropped. I had forgotten some of the twists in the story, which made it even more fun, and I was happy with the ending, which I had conveniently forgotten.<br/><br/>I can't remember how old I was when I first read this book, but I do remember loving it so much that I wrote a letter to the author on an el</span>
  <span id="freeText10315808100236071795" style="display:none">This was one of my favorite children's books, and I decided to reread it by listening to it on audio. What a delight! The mystery held up really well, and Diane the Adult had fun following the clues being dropped. I had forgotten some of the twists in the story, which made it even more fun, and I was happy with the ending, which I had conveniently forgotten.<br /><br />I can't remember how old I was when I first read this book, but I do remember loving it so much that I wrote a letter to the author on an electric typewriter. However, when I looked at Ellen Raskin's bio page on Wikipedia, it says she died in 1984 when she was just 56, so she was likely already gone while I was typing out my admiration for her work. Ellen, wherever you are, thanks for writing such a clever book for children. When I was young, reading that book made me feel like a grownup, and as an adult, reading the book made me feel like a kid again. Cheers to you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10315808100236071795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78783288?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="seth" href="/user/show/2963555-seth">seth</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78783288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16583901131536607420">Unless you love driving chainsaws through your innards, please do not read The Westing Game. The Westing Game, by Ellen Raskin, is an extremely horrible clue-chasing mystery. The books main events take place in a 5-story apartment called Sunset Towers and the Westing Estate. The book takes place in Michigan around 1975. When reading the Westing Game, it is no more fun than jumping off a cliff for fun. <br/>Barney Northrup, a sixty-two year old salesman, is selling apartment spaces in Sunset Towers to</span>
  <span id="freeText16583901131536607420" style="display:none">Unless you love driving chainsaws through your innards, please do not read The Westing Game. The Westing Game, by Ellen Raskin, is an extremely horrible clue-chasing mystery. The books main events take place in a 5-story apartment called Sunset Towers and the Westing Estate. The book takes place in Michigan around 1975. When reading the Westing Game, it is no more fun than jumping off a cliff for fun. <br />Barney Northrup, a sixty-two year old salesman, is selling apartment spaces in Sunset Towers to secretly selected sixteen people. These people all have connections with Samuel W. Westing and are his heirs. Soon after the sixteen settle in to Sunset Towers, Samuel W. Westing is presumed dead. The heirs are all called to the library in the Westing Estate for the reading of the will. Although the sixteen heirs are considered the main characters, the book focuses mainly on the Wexler family. <br />Grace Wexler is married to Jake Wexler, and they have two kids, Angela and Turtle. Grace is house decorator in her mid-forties. Jake is a podiatrist who now works in the lobby of Sunset Towers, since they moved there. Angela is a very calm person who is currently engaged to Denton D. Deere, also a doctor. <br />	Overall, the reason I really I really disliked this book was because how boring it was. I was literally sitting there turning a page every five minutes! I would not recommend this to anyone. Over the weeks, the clues still puzzle the heirs, and many events occur including bombs, injuries, parties, and more! Read The Westing Game (don’t) to find out what really happens. If you do choose to read The Westing Game , they have places for people like you. <br /><br /><br /> <br /></span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1309579576491646223">This... iconic book. This may be one of the books I've reread most in my life. I love this book so much. <br/><br/>I don't even know what to say about this book other than it's one of my favorites ever. It somehow fits every age group - I appreciate it just as much now as when I was ten. <br/><br/>The Westing Game is a brilliant logic puzzle. It's a mystery with all the clues lined up, but you won't get the solution. Trust me, you won't. <b>You'll just scream at the end when you figure everything out.</b> I'm shocked that</span>
  <span id="freeText1309579576491646223" style="display:none">This... iconic book. This may be one of the books I've reread most in my life. I love this book so much. <br /><br />I don't even know what to say about this book other than it's one of my favorites ever. It somehow fits every age group - I appreciate it just as much now as when I was ten. <br /><br />The Westing Game is a brilliant logic puzzle. It's a mystery with all the clues lined up, but you won't get the solution. Trust me, you won't. <b>You'll just scream at the end when you figure everything out.</b> I'm shocked that any author was able to develop the solution and set everything up so well. <b>Every time I read this, I notice a new clue.</b><br /><br />This book also succeeds because of its well-rounded characters. Turtle, Angela, Flora, JJ, and Ted are all well-developed, interesting characters. Frankly, all the characters are interesting and memorable, even with so many. <br /><br />Recommended for MG and YA readers alike. </span>

My life was taken by me-by one of you!...Cast out the sinner, let the guilty rise and confess. Who among you is worthy to be the Westing heir?"</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br><b>16 UNLIKELY HEIRS ON THE HUNT OF A MURDERER AMONG THEM?</b> The Westing Game begins.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1530953869ra/25936445.gif" width="500" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I never would have thought that this is a children's book. Because honestly, the ingenuity of the plot transcends all age-bracket genres. <b>THIS IS A MASTERPIECE.</b> Sure this isn't really perfect but danggg son, <b>IT'S THE FCKING TWISTS THAT DID ME.</b> At first I just read on without giving any thought about the "murderer" then by the latter part I'm going to piece off one theory, then it get's utterly crushed, and OH! here's another more plausible one, harharrr wrong again! Then BOOM everything is laid out perfectly right under my nose. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1415275290ra/11762644.gif" width="500" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>What I loved about this book aside from the eye-catching plot, is how these 16 heirs were fleshed out vividly and had developed by the end of the book. I was satisfied that we readers still get to see these 16 heirs grow and age. YES. The conclusion gave us glimpses of their futures in snippets even after The Westing Game had finally resolved. I just love these endings.<br><br>Overall, this book was filled with humor and witty dialogues. I loved how I pined for the right character! HAAHHA. I recommend this to everyone who wants a fast yet enjoyable mystery book. Definitely one of my fave books this 2018. <br><br>P.S. <div id="review_3075059349" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3075059349" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1376766-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582132250p2/1376766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3075059349?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1376766-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=mystery-and-thrillers">mystery-and-thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=disappointing">disappointing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=ebook_kindle">ebook_kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=childrens-books">childrens-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3075059349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4798915215537462591">I read this with some friends who eschew pantalones. I'd never heard of this book before, despite it being a book for children that was available when I was a child. I think, had I read this way back when, I likely would have enjoyed it a lot more than I did reading it now as a woman who once rode a dinosaur around town, probably. <br/><br/>The premise is such: A very rich man leaves a will naming 16 people as his heirs, but only the one who solves The Mystery(tm) will inherit all of the money that exists</span>
  <span id="freeText4798915215537462591" style="display:none">I read this with some friends who eschew pantalones. I'd never heard of this book before, despite it being a book for children that was available when I was a child. I think, had I read this way back when, I likely would have enjoyed it a lot more than I did reading it now as a woman who once rode a dinosaur around town, probably. <br /><br />The premise is such: A very rich man leaves a will naming 16 people as his heirs, but only the one who solves The Mystery(tm) will inherit all of the money that exists. Nominally $200 million. <br /><br />There's a lot going on in this book. There's a diverse cast. There's a mystery. There's... a plot. Of sorts. <br /><br />But what there isn't, at least in my opinion, is cohesion and sense. This book has an ever shifting 3rd person limited Point-of-view narrative structure, sort of stream of consciousness, if we (the reader) existed in the story as a telepathic fly. We flit around and pick up snippets of conversation and thoughts... but the fly has the advantage over us because at least the fly would know whose head it has landed on at the moment. As a reader, there are a lot of ownerless thoughts just hanging out in this book, and it bugged me. (See what I did there?) <br /><br />On top of that, this book makes use of the dialogue as action style that I really loathe, AND it lacks narrative transitional structure. So one minute we'll have someone saying "Go upstairs and say hello." and then the next line is the hello - not even an "OK, sure". <br /><br />At one point, one of the characters is in the hospital recovering from an injury. Throughout the time that this character is there, characters just show up, and then show up back at their apartment complex randomly, as though the hospital is IN the building - which it is not. Including a 13 year old child. Just "Oh, we're at this place now. Annnnnd now we're not again?" <br /><br />It was just very difficult for me to track what was happening, where we were, how much time had passed in the story, who was speaking or thinking at the time. It was just a jumble of words surrounding a mystery that all of the characters were trying to solve... and not one bit of it made sense to me. <br /><br />The mystery laid out in the will was not as it seemed, and the real solution was to a question that was never asked and a mystery that was never mentioned. It all seemed laid out and planned from the outset, and the level of prep-work required would have been astronomical. And I'm left feeling confused and dissatisfied with the end of the book because it seems like a huge amount of work... for nothing. The end result could have been accomplished much, much, much easier without all the subterfuge and trickery, and it would have actually been much more kind as well. <br /><br />I just... didn't get this one. It didn't work for me. For all that it's less than 200 pages, it took me AGES to read it because the style just killed my interest every time I picked it up. I was determined to finish, and so I did, but I don't feel like it was worth it. It's a shame, because this book has great reviews, and I had hoped to enjoy it, but it wasn't to be.<br /><br />2 stars for the potential had I read it when I was an appropriate age and not ancient.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4798915215537462591" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1829806981212831783">As a child, I probably read this book as many times as I watched the movie "Clue" (brilliance), and that is a lot! I loved (and still love) anything with a clever girl as a protagonist. Turtle can stand her ground among Nancy Drew and her ilk. Raskin's cast of characters feels somehow simultaneously real and fantastical, and the mystery is juicy enough to keep you hooked until the final moment of checkmate.</span>

<div id="review_2623809197" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2623809197" />
    <a title="Madeline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111921-madeline"><img alt="Madeline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2623809197?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madeline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madeline" href="/user/show/111921-madeline">Madeline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=kids-and-young-adult">kids-and-young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2623809197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18250338945239877783">I checked out this audiobook on a whim when I saw that it was available, because it seemed like a quick, fun nostalgia read. I remember being assigned to read this book in fifth grade or sixth grade, and had fond memories of it as a brief, fun little puzzle of a story. <br/><br/><i>The Westing Game</i> begins when sixteen people are called to the abandoned Westing mansion to hear the will of Sam Westing, recently deceased millionaire industrialist. In his will, Westing proposes a game: the sixteen people (his “h</span>
  <span id="freeText18250338945239877783" style="display:none">I checked out this audiobook on a whim when I saw that it was available, because it seemed like a quick, fun nostalgia read. I remember being assigned to read this book in fifth grade or sixth grade, and had fond memories of it as a brief, fun little puzzle of a story. <br /><br /><i>The Westing Game</i> begins when sixteen people are called to the abandoned Westing mansion to hear the will of Sam Westing, recently deceased millionaire industrialist. In his will, Westing proposes a game: the sixteen people (his “heirs”) will be divided into teams of two and given a handful of clues, which they must use to figure out who murdered Westing. The team that wins inherits his entire fortune. <br /><br />Honestly, on re-reading this, I realize that it’s basically <i>Saw</i> for the elementary school set: rich eccentric dude brings a group of strangers together and proceeds to psychologically torture them by a) teasing them with the chance to win an outrageous fortune and b) convincing them that someone in their group is a murderer. Plus there’s puns and puzzles based on patriotic songs! <br /><br />In short, this has not aged well. Maybe people were more open to the idea of a rich guy fucking with people’s lives for shits and giggles back when the book was originally published in 1979, but reading <i>The Westing Game</i> in the year of our lord 2018 was a significantly different experience for me. Watching all of these people go through what was basically an elaborate parlor game to appease the whims of a rich dead asshole wasn’t very fun, at all, and it was a genuine disappointment for me when at the end <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[it turns out that not only was Westing alive the entire time, none of the characters ever take him to task for how thoroughly he disrupted their lives for his own amusement <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />And it’s not just the general plot that left a bad taste in my mouth – there are a lot of little things that I definitely didn’t realize were problematic when I read the book as a kid. A child with mental disabilities is described by a character as “a mongoloid child”; a Chinese woman’s inner thoughts are written in broken English; and the one black character wears traditional African clothing once, but those are all the details about it we get, since no country or other information about her clothes are provided, aside from a character calling her outfit “ethnic” and the narration describing her as looking like “an African princess.” Oof. Oh, and since one of the characters has Down’s Syndrome, listening to the audiobook meant having to listen to a non-disabled voice actor do an impression of a person with a speech impediment, which…was not fun for me. I mean, I don’t know how else they were supposed to do it, but that doesn’t make it any easier to listen to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18250338945239877783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6065334556233707581">This should seriously be added to the annals of “The World’s Most Brilliant, Strange Books”. I "solved" the mystery twice and was still wrong both times. :D I even had what I thought was a major spoiler to help me. I was still wrong! But in the end all the twists and turns made perfect sense, and I was left wondering how I hadn’t seen it all before. Also, this book has a grand total of sixteen point of view characters! I would usually call that a bad thing, but this author somehow made me care (</span>
  <span id="freeText6065334556233707581" style="display:none">This should seriously be added to the annals of “The World’s Most Brilliant, Strange Books”. I "solved" the mystery twice and was still wrong both times. :D I even had what I thought was a major spoiler to help me. I was still wrong! But in the end all the twists and turns made perfect sense, and I was left wondering how I hadn’t seen it all before. Also, this book has a grand total of sixteen point of view characters! I would usually call that a bad thing, but this author somehow made me care (sometimes even love) each character and recognize them by name. ;) A+ for character development!! <br /><br />I enjoyed the clever clues, the even greater mystery lurking in the shadows, and the quirky cast of characters. Jake Wexler and Theo were my absolute favorites. :D It was really neat to see how, even if there was only one winner to the game, everyone’s lives were brightened and changed by their participation. And I really liked the end. &lt;3 I cried over all the sweetness and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the emptiness of one character’s life <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />Just a note that there are some bad attitudes among the children, a bit of slight feminism, and a very theatrically descriptive tall tale about a corpse that is repeated several times during the opening of the book. <br /><br />Best quote: “I remember the will said, 'May God thy gold refine.' That must be from the Bible."<br />"Shakespeare," Turtle said. All quotations were either from the Bible or Shakespeare.<br /><br />Altogether, I very much enjoyed my jaunt into this quirky, brilliant book!</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3087912287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer687360176703375744">Entertaining solve the puzzle win the prize mystery.<br/><br/>Here is a book that is very, very good when you view it from within its genre's boundaries, Children's Literature, but if you try and analyze it outside of it then it starts to fall apart. Told from Turtle's perspective, prepubescent female, we are pulled into the game of the town's richest citizen, Sam Westing. He's dead and one of the players present was responsible! <br/><br/>Solve the mystery and win his fortune. <br/><br/>There's a good deal of point-of-view </span>
  <span id="freeText687360176703375744" style="display:none">Entertaining solve the puzzle win the prize mystery.<br /><br />Here is a book that is very, very good when you view it from within its genre's boundaries, Children's Literature, but if you try and analyze it outside of it then it starts to fall apart. Told from Turtle's perspective, prepubescent female, we are pulled into the game of the town's richest citizen, Sam Westing. He's dead and one of the players present was responsible! <br /><br />Solve the mystery and win his fortune. <br /><br />There's a good deal of point-of-view switching to keep the reader disoriented and guessing while also providing break points for younger readers. At first, the characters seem like gross stereotypes, but these quickly change as Raskin reveals hidden qualities that many would overlook at just a glance; providing the 'Don't judge a book by it's cover' or 'Don't judge a person by the way they look' parable.<br /><br />The mystery is good and tricky. But the most unexpected part of the book was the ending and how emotional it was; giving context and closure to what was a game based on avarice. <br /><br />Thanks for the buddy read, and for pleasant dive into a genre I generally avoid.</span>

<div id="review_20720733" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20720733" />
    <a title="Tung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/763514-tung"><img alt="Tung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199981516p2/763514.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20720733?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tung" href="/user/show/763514-tung">Tung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763514-tung?shelf=children">children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20720733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6042411776902823811">Yes, this is a children’s book – a Newbery Medal winner from 1978. This was also my absolute favorite book from when I read it as a third grader until I was in middle school and discovered fantasy fiction. I saw it in a used bookstore and decided to press my luck and re-read it, hoping it wouldn’t disappoint me as other childhood favorites had done upon a re-reading (I’m looking at you, Hitchhiker’s Guide…). It didn’t. The Westing Game begins with sixteen seemingly random individuals invited to </span>
  <span id="freeText6042411776902823811" style="display:none">Yes, this is a children’s book – a Newbery Medal winner from 1978. This was also my absolute favorite book from when I read it as a third grader until I was in middle school and discovered fantasy fiction. I saw it in a used bookstore and decided to press my luck and re-read it, hoping it wouldn’t disappoint me as other childhood favorites had done upon a re-reading (I’m looking at you, Hitchhiker’s Guide…). It didn’t. The Westing Game begins with sixteen seemingly random individuals invited to live in a beautiful building called Sunset Towers. The individuals are then invited to the reading of the will of the wealthy Samuel Westing, recently found dead. But rather than a standard will reading, the sixteen individuals are divided into pairs and given envelopes filled with random words from which they must decipher who the murderer of Samuel Westing is. Winner receives $200 million, and the game is on. The rest of the book details the connection of the individuals to each other, and the revelation of each of the team’s sets of clues. Two decades later in a re-read, and the solution to the mystery is far more obvious than it was to me in the third grade. But the way Raskin unfolds each set of clues, and finds plausible ways to attempt to mislead the reader with the clues, and two decades later, I can better appreciate her wordplay. A definite must-read for elementary school kids, and a recommended read for adults who missed reading this gem when they were in school. Still one of my favorites.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6042411776902823811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('b5U1RfHhb/i6sNCCCt/+mbFBg9jxx3Y2Qp3/+dxR+fFVs1yXSr+mkB2bZOP9KvRGZx5kED9K+FEZ47lIERILaw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Vlbpy7/pAaMZuEPheFCeZgEMAMENi4e+ZC/FVzCBA7lscIAZBLfIy76T94CPpZS511PnCcMGCdk/UYPm/cLxIw==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/10074.Ellen_Raskin">About Ellen Raskin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/10074.Ellen_Raskin"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1215658052p3/10074.jpg);'></div>

</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14503147185603135180">Ellen Raskin was a writer, illustrator, and designer. She was born in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, and grew up during the Great Depression. She primarily wrote for children. She received the 1979 Newbery Medal for her 1978 book, <i>The Westing Game</i>.<br/><br/>Ms. Raskin was also an accomplished graphic artist. She designed dozens of dust jackets for books, including the first edition of Madeleine L'Engle's classic <i>A W</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14503147185603135180" style="display:none">Ellen Raskin was a writer, illustrator, and designer. She was born in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, and grew up during the Great Depression. She primarily wrote for children. She received the 1979 Newbery Medal for her 1978 book, <i>The Westing Game</i>.<br /><br />Ms. Raskin was also an accomplished graphic artist. She designed dozens of dust jackets for books, including the first edition of Madeleine L'Engle's classic <i>A Wrinkle in Time</i>.<br /><br />She married Dennis Flanagan, editor of <i>Scientific American</i>, in 1965.<br /><br />Raskin died at the age of 56 on August 8, 1984, in New York City due to complications from connective tissue disease.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14503147185603135180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
